## Domain Knowledge
**Domain**: classical mechanics (nonlinear oscillations / dynamical systems)
**Analysis**: The target dv_dt is acceleration, with state variables x (position) and v (velocity), matching a second-order ODE for an oscillator. Nonlinear harmonic oscillators commonly model dv_dt as sums of restoring forces in x, damping forces in v, and optional time-dependent (driving/parametric) forcing in t.

**Common formula structures** (built-in generic + LLM domain-specific):
- Arrhenius equation: k = c0 * exp(c1 / T) (chemical reaction rate dependence on temperature)
- Coulomb's / Newton's inverse-square law: F = c0 / r**2 (gravitational or electrostatic force)
- Stefan-Boltzmann law: P = c0 * T**4 (blackbody radiation power vs temperature)
- Beer-Lambert law: I = c0 * exp(c1 * x) (light intensity attenuation through a medium)
- Michaelis-Menten kinetics: v = c0 * S / (S + c1) (enzyme-catalyzed reaction saturation)
- Hill equation: y = c0 * x**c1 / (x**c1 + c2) (cooperative binding, sigmoidal dose-response)
- Kepler's third law: T**2 = c0 * a**3 (orbital period vs semi-major axis)
- Newton's law of cooling: T = c0 + c1 * exp(c2 * t) (exponential thermal relaxation)
- Clausius-Clapeyron relation: log(P) = c0 + c1 / T (vapor pressure vs temperature)
- Langmuir adsorption isotherm: theta = c0 * P / (1 + c1 * P) (surface adsorption equilibrium)
- Freundlich isotherm: q = c0 * C**c1 (empirical adsorption on heterogeneous surface)
- Stokes' drag: F = c0 * r * v (viscous drag force on a sphere in fluid)
- Logistic growth: N = c0 / (1 + c1 * exp(c2 * t)) (population dynamics with carrying capacity)
- Planck-Wien approximation: I = c0 * f**3 * exp(c1 * f / T) (thermal radiation spectrum)
- Linear harmonic oscillator (undamped, unforced): dv_dt = c0 * x (restoring acceleration proportional to displacement; sign absorbed in c0)
- Linear viscous damping + spring: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * v (mass-normalized spring and damping terms)
- Constant/biased forcing: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * v + c2 (equilibrium shifted by constant load)
- Duffing oscillator (cubic stiffness): dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**3 (hardening/softening spring nonlinearity)
- Duffing with damping: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**3 + c2 * v (common nonlinear oscillator benchmark)
- Quadratic (speed) drag damping: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * v + c2 * v * Abs(v) (aerodynamic-like damping in 1D motion)
- Rayleigh-type nonlinear damping: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * v + c2 * v**3 (amplitude-dependent damping/anti-damping)
- van der Pol oscillator form: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * (c2 + c3 * x**2) * v (self-excited oscillation via negative damping at small |x|)
- Pendulum-like restoring force (sinusoidal): dv_dt = c0 * sin(c1 * x) + c2 * v (large-angle restoring force approximation)
- Nonlinear stiffness with saturation: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x / (c2 + x**2) + c3 * v (restoring force that flattens at large |x|)
- Dry (Coulomb) friction + spring: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * v + c2 * sign(v) (stick-slip prone acceleration law)
- Harmonic driving: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * v + c2 * sin(c3 * t) (externally forced nonlinear/linear oscillator)
- Parametric excitation: dv_dt = (c0 + c1 * cos(c2 * t)) * x + c3 * v (time-varying stiffness leading to Mathieu-type behavior)
- General polynomial normal form: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * v + c2 * x**2 + c3 * x**3 + c4 * x * v + c5 * v**2 (local approximation of smooth nonlinear dynamics)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope â†’ Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects â†’ Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay â†’ Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior â†’ Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects â†’ Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- Conservative (no dissipation) dynamics â†’ dv_dt depends primarily on x (and not on v); restoring term often odd in x (e.g., x, x**3, sin(c*x)) implying symmetric potential.
- Dissipation â†’ dv_dt includes terms proportional to v or nonlinear in v (v, v**3, v*Abs(v)); these typically oppose motion (effective coefficient negative).
- Self-excited limit cycles â†’ damping coefficient changes sign with amplitude/state, e.g., (c0 + c1 * x**2) * v (negative damping near origin, positive at large |x|).
- Hardening/softening stiffness â†’ presence of x**3 (or higher odd powers) shifts oscillation frequency with amplitude; sign of the cubic term determines hardening vs softening.
- Dry friction / stick-slip â†’ sign(v) or piecewise dependence on v produces nonsmooth acceleration and can create flat spots/plateaus in v(t).
- External periodic forcing â†’ explicit t-dependence via sin(c*t) or cos(c*t) introduces sustained oscillations and resonance peaks in dv_dt patterns.
- Parametric resonance â†’ time-modulated stiffness multiplying x (e.g., (c0 + c1*cos(c2*t))*x) can create growth near specific frequency ratios even without additive forcing.
- Velocity-position coupling â†’ cross terms like x*v indicate non-conservative/gyroscopic-like effects or reduced-order models with state-dependent damping/stiffness.
- Saturation of restoring force â†’ rational forms like x/(c0 + x**2) or bounded functions like tanh(c*x) indicate finite-force limits at large displacement.

I need to determine if any candidate formula can match the GT: `-1.08868519129651*x**3 - 0.600404533977091*Abs(v)**0.333333333333333`

The key challenge is the `Abs(v)**0.333333333333333` term. Since v can be negative (physics oscillator), `Abs(v)**0.333333333333333` is NOT the same as `v**0.333333333333333`. For negative v, `v**(1/3)` gives a real cube root (negative), while `Abs(v)**(1/3)` is always positive.

So I need a candidate that can produce:
1. A term `-c * x**3` (easy)
2. A term `-c * |v|^{1/3}` (requires absolute value or equivalent construction)

None of the candidates explicitly contain `Abs(v)`. Let me check if any combination of terms with `v` can replicate `|v|^{1/3}`.

**Key observation:** `|v|^{1/3}` is an even function of v (symmetric about v=0). Meanwhile, `v^{1/3}` is an odd function. No finite sum of power functions `v^a` (for various real exponents a) can produce `|v|^{1/3}` because power functions of v are either even (integer even exponents) or odd (integer odd exponents), or undefined for negative v (non-integer exponents in standard real analysis).

For non-integer exponents like `v**c` in the candidates, when v is negative, these are generally undefined in real arithmetic (or complex-valued). So `v**(1/3)` would need special handling.

However, in many symbolic math systems, `v**(1/3)` for negative v returns the real cube root, which is `-|v|^{1/3}`. So `v**(1/3)` is odd, not even like `|v|^{1/3}`.

Could we combine terms to get `|v|^{1/3}`? Note that `|v|^{1/3} = sign(v) * v^{1/3}` when using real cube roots... no, actually `|v|^{1/3}` is always positive, while `v^{1/3}` (real) has the sign of v.

We could try: Can `c1*v^a + c2*v^b + c3*v^2 + ...` equal `-0.6|v|^{1/3}`?

For even functions: `v^2` is even, but `v^{1/3}` is odd. To create an even function from odd-powered terms, we'd need cancellation that works for all v, which isn't possible with a finite number of power-law terms of different exponents.

**What about sin terms?** `sin(c*v)` is odd in v, so it can't help create an even function either. Combining `sin` with power terms still can't produce `|v|^{1/3}`.

**What about candidates with `v**2` terms?** Setting `v**2` coefficient to 0 removes it, but that doesn't help create `|v|^{1/3}`.

**Could `v**c` be interpreted as `|v|**c` in some systems?** This is a critical question. In some symbolic regression frameworks, `v**c` for non-integer c might be implemented as `|v|**c` to avoid complex numbers. If that's the case, then many candidates could work.

If we assume `v**c` means `|v|**c` (a common convention in symbolic regression to keep things real-valued), then candidates with multiple `v**c` terms could work by setting extra terms to zero.

For example, **Candidate 25**: `c0 + c1*x**3 + c2*v**2 + c3*v**c4 + c5*v**c6 + v**c7`
Set c0=0, c1=-1.08869, c2=0, c3=-0.60040, c4=1/3, c5=0, c7=large even integer (making v**c7 negligible)... but `v**c7` has no coefficient to zero out.

Actually, set c7 to 0: `v**0 = 1`, then adjust c0 to compensate: c0 = -1. Then: `-1 + c1*x**3 + c3*|v|^{1/3} + 1 = c1*x**3 + c3*|v|^{1/3}`. This works!

Set c0=-1, c1=-1.08869, c2=0, c3=-0.60040, c4=1/3, c5=0, c6=anything, c7=0. Then the expression = -1 + c1*x**3 + 0 + c3*|v|^{1/3} + 0 + 1 = c1*x**3 + c3*|v|^{1/3}. âœ“

This matches if `v**c` is interpreted as `|v|**c`. Given the physics context and symbolic regression conventions, this is standard.

Candidate 25 works, and so do many others with similar structure.
